### Question 44: How does the core quest course selection work to fulfill core credits?  
### Answer: 

The core QUEST course selection consists of four pre-assigned courses: Quantitative Reasoning for a Digital Age (4 credits), Scientific Inquiry (4 credits), Introduction to Statistics (4 credits), and Programming for Data Science and Visualization (4 credits). These courses are designed to provide students with a strong foundation in quantitative reasoning, scientific inquiry, statistics, and data science and visualization skills. They are assigned to students in their first year or the first semester of the second year, ensuring that all students receive a comprehensive introduction to these essential areas of study. These courses collectively contribute 16 credits towards the total core requirement of 20 credits for Class of 2027 students and earlier. The core QUEST courses are integral to the curriculum, aiming to equip students with the quantitative and analytical skills necessary for success in their academic and professional pursuits.

### Question 45: Can I take a 300-level course to fulfill a 200-level major requirement?  
### Answer: 

No, according to the provided context, students are required to take six (06) 200-level intermediate courses for their major requirements. The context specifies that students must take 200-level courses to satisfy the major requirements, and it does not mention the possibility of using 300-level courses to fulfill these requirements. The note about cross-listed or tagged courses states that up to 8 credits (two courses) can be used to satisfy both major and minor requirements, but this is limited to cross-listed or tagged courses and does not apply to the general fulfillment of 200-level major requirements with 300-level courses. Therefore, it is irrelevant to the question to mention the possibility of double-counting courses for majors and minors, as this does not address the direct question of using a 300-level course to fulfill a 200-level major requirement. The context clearly outlines the requirements for major courses, and there is no indication that a 300-level course can be used to satisfy a 200-level major requirement.

### Question 46: If I take a course at another university and want to transfer the credits to Fulbright, but Fulbright doesn’t have that major code, yet the course is related to a major at Fulbright, can I count it toward that major?  
### Answer: 

When applying for Credit Transfer, a student is responsible for providing all the information that is requested about the course(s) for which they are applying to transfer. Information may include but not limited to course curriculum, learning outcomes, course content, and assessments. To apply for Credit Transfer or an Exemption, students should submit an Application for Credit Transfer, along with the required information on the course(s), to the Office of the Registrar. The Registrar will coordinate with the Office of the Dean to determine whether credit or an exemption is justified. So, if you take a course at another university and want to transfer the credits to Fulbright but Fulbright doesn't have that major code, you can still apply for Credit Transfer. You need to provide all the necessary information about the course, such as curriculum, learning outcomes, course content, and assessments, to support your application. The Office of the Registrar and the Office of the Dean will then evaluate the course to determine if it can be counted toward a major at Fulbright. It's important to note that the course must be equivalent to a course at Fulbright, and a maximum of 64 credit hours can be transferred and credited to a Fulbright undergraduate degree.  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>  

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 20]

Students will declare their major after completion of the Core and Exploratory requirements. Students also need to fulfill the specific requirements (if any) of the major. All courses used to fulfill major or minor requirements must be taken for a letter grade. Students can claim a maximum of 8 credits (2 courses) toward both their Exploratory and major requirements. For example, an exploratory course can also be counted as a major course for the corresponding major. However, the number of double-counted credits is often limited (e.g., see subsections 13.2 and 13.3). Additionally, any particular course can only be used to satisfy at most two requirements. For instance, if course X is cross-listed between major A and major B and tagged by minor C, then X can be used to fulfill the requirements of major A and major B, or major A and minor C, or major B and minor C. Triple counting is not allowed. 13.9  Maximal Length of Study Fulbright follows MOET regulations, which state that the maximum time to complete the program cannot exceed double the standard

### Question 47: I have 112 credits from Fulbright and 16 credits from EL. Can I graduate?  
### Answer: 

To graduate from Fulbright University Vietnam, you need to have at least 128 earned credits. You currently have 112 credits from Fulbright and 16 credits from Experiential Learning (EL), which totals 128 credits. Therefore, you have met the minimum requirement for earned credits to graduate. However, it's important to ensure that all other graduation requirements, such as completing the Core and Exploratory requirements, specific major requirements, and any other university-specific requirements, are also fulfilled. Always check with your academic advisor to confirm that all aspects of your academic program have been completed satisfactorily.

### Question 50: In a regular semester, can I take only 2 courses? If so, how will my tuition fees be affected?  
### Answer: 

Yes, you can take only 2 courses in a regular semester. However, it's important to note that withdrawing from courses to reduce your course load below 12 credits may affect your financial aid eligibility, as you need to maintain full-time enrollment (at least 12 credits) to qualify for most types of financial aid. Regarding tuition fees, no reduction in tuition is granted to students whose course withdrawal results in fewer than 12 academic credits. Therefore, you will still be charged the full tuition for the courses you are enrolled in, even if you withdraw from one or both of them. It's crucial to be aware of these financial implications before deciding to take fewer than the standard course load. Additionally, to officially withdraw from a course, you must submit the Course Withdrawal form to the Registrar's Office within the specified timeline. If you're considering this option, it's advisable to consult with the financial aid office and your academic advisor to fully understand the implications on your financial aid, academic progress, and degree completion timeline.  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>  

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 7]
maintaining the full-time enrollment status of registering for a minimum of 12 credits per term. • Course Withdrawal: There are two Withdrawal periods per term. The first period starts after 4:00 PM Friday, Week 2 to 4:00 PM Friday, Week 4. During this time, students can request to withdraw from the course without any consequence. The second period starts after 4:00 PM Friday, Week 4 to 4:00 PM Friday, Week 8. Attempted credits and a “W” grade will be recorded on the transcript for the course(s) from which students withdraw in this period. This grade does not affect a student’s GPA. In order to withdraw from a course, students need to submit the Course Withdrawal form to the Registrar’s Office, following the above timeline. A student who withdraws from a course beyond the halfway point of that course (end of week 4 for an 8-week course or end of week 8 for a full-term course) automatically receives a grade of ‘F’, which will affect the student’s GPA. Exceptions will only be permitted in exceptional

### Question 51: Can I pursue 3 majors?  
### Answer: 

No, the text does not mention anything about pursuing three majors. It only discusses pursuing a single major or double major. The text states that students pursuing a double major must complete all requirements for each major and that up to 8 credits (two courses) can be used to satisfy both sets of requirements if the two majors share cross-listed or tagged courses. It also explains the Exploratory requirements and how they interact with major requirements, but there is no mention of pursuing three majors simultaneously. Therefore, based on the given information, it is not possible to pursue three majors. The rules and limits provided are specifically for single majors and double majors, not triple majors.

### Question 52: How can I change my major declaration?  
### Answer: 

To change your major declaration, you should follow these steps:

1. **Meet with the Major Coordinator**: The first step is to meet with the major coordinator of the new major you are interested in. They can provide you with detailed information about the requirements, courses, and any additional steps you need to take.

2. **Review the Major Requirements**: Make sure you understand the requirements for the new major, including the number of courses, specific courses you need to take, and any prerequisites.

3. **Plan Your Course Schedule**: Adjust your course schedule to accommodate the new major requirements. This may involve dropping or adding courses.

4. **Update Your Academic Record**: You will need to formally declare the new major through the university's academic advising or registration system. This may involve filling out a change of major form or meeting with an academic advisor.

5. **Seek Approval if Necessary**: Some majors may require additional approvals, such as from the Dean's Office or specific faculty members. Make sure to follow the procedures outlined in the major's description and flowchart or handbook.

6. **Consider Implications**: Changing your major can have various implications, such as extending your time to graduation or affecting financial aid. Make sure to consider these factors before making a decision.

7. **Stay Informed**: Keep yourself informed about the new major's policies and requirements, and stay in touch with your academic advisor for guidance and support throughout the process.

Remember, changing your major is a significant decision and should be made after careful consideration of your academic and career goals. It's also important to stay in close communication with your advisors to ensure a smooth transition to your new major.

### Question 53: What is the process of delaying my payment?  
### Answer: 

In the context provided, delaying payment would not be a typical process as it pertains to academic settings and procedures. However, if a student faces exceptional circumstances that prevent them from making a payment on time, they may request special considerations. Here's how the process might unfold based on the given contexts:

1. **Identify Exceptional Circumstances**: The student must first identify circumstances beyond their control that prevent them from making a payment on time. These could include unforeseeable events such as relationship breakdown, financial difficulties, or disabilities for which reasonable adjustments have been made.

2. **Gather Supporting Documentation**: The student should gather any relevant documentation to support their claim. This could include medical records, bank statements, or other evidence of the financial hardship.

3. **Contact the Instructor or Academic Advisor**: The student should contact their instructor or academic advisor to discuss the situation and request a delay in payment. It is important to do this as early as possible, ideally before the payment deadline.

4. **Submit a Request for Special Consideration**: The student should submit a formal request for special consideration to the appropriate office, which could be the Registrar's Office or the Financial Aid Office, depending on the institution's policies.

5. **Follow the Institution's Procedures**: The institution will have specific procedures for handling such requests. This may involve filling out a specific form, providing all necessary documentation, and possibly meeting with a committee to review the request.

6. **Await Decision**: The student must wait for a decision from the institution. The institution will review the request and supporting documentation and decide whether to grant a delay in payment or provide alternative arrangements.

7. **Agree to Terms**: If the request is granted, the student must agree to any terms set by the institution, such as a payment plan or other conditions.

It is important to note that the institution's policies and procedures will dictate the exact steps and timelines involved in this process. The student should refer to their institution's academic calendar, student handbook, or financial aid office for specific guidance.

**Irrelevant References**: The provided contexts do not directly address payment delays but rather focus on academic accommodations, grade appeals, course withdrawals, and waitlisting procedures. These are separate processes from delaying payment and do not apply directly to the question of payment delays.

**Final Answer**: The process of delaying payment due to exceptional circumstances involves identifying the circumstances, gathering supporting documentation, contacting the instructor or academic advisor, submitting a formal request for special consideration to the appropriate office, following the institution's procedures, awaiting a decision, and agreeing to any terms set by the institution.

### Question 55: How many courses can I take in total?  
### Answer: 

The total number of courses a student can take is not explicitly limited by the provided text. However, the text does provide several constraints and requirements that must be considered when planning a course load:

1. **Exploratory Requirements**: Students must complete 8 credits (2 courses) from each of three categories (Arts and Humanities, Social Sciences, STEM). No course can be taken to fulfill more than one category.

2. **Experiential Learning Requirements**: Students must complete 5 core courses (20 credits).

3. **Double Counting**: Up to 8 credits (2 courses) can be used to satisfy both major and minor requirements. This limit is per each pair of majors (or major and minor). Additionally, up to 8 exploratory credits (2 courses) can be counted towards the major requirements.

4. **Double Counting Limit**: Any particular course can only be used to satisfy at most two requirements. For example, a cross-listed or tagged course can be used to fulfill the requirements of two majors or a major and a minor, but not more than that.

5. **Maximum Credits**: The total number of credits required for graduation is 128.

Given these constraints, a student can take a variety of courses to meet these requirements, but the total number of courses (not credits) is not directly specified. The key is to ensure that the combination of courses meets all the outlined requirements without exceeding the 128-credit limit.

To calculate the total number of courses a student might take, consider:

- 5 core courses (20 credits)
- 6 exploratory courses (24 credits, 3 courses from each category)
- Courses for the major(s) and minor(s), with up to 8 credits (2 courses) double-counted.

The exact number of courses will depend on how many courses are needed to meet the major and minor requirements, and how many of those courses can be double-counted.

**Note**: The text does not provide a specific upper limit on the total number of courses a student can take, only the constraints on how those courses can be used to meet various requirements. Therefore, the answer to the question "How many courses can I take in total?" is not directly provided by the text, but the student must ensure that all requirements are met within the 128-credit limit.

### Question 56: How many courses in a semester can I take in total?  
### Answer: 

The total number of courses a student can take in a semester is not explicitly stated in the given contexts. However, the information provided does give some guidelines on course selection and requirements:

- Exploratory requirements: Students need to complete 8 credits (2 courses at the 100-level or 200-level) from each of three categories (Arts and Humanities, Social Sciences, STEM), totaling 24 credits (6 courses) for the exploratory requirements.
- Core courses: Students need to complete 5 core courses (20 credits).
- Major requirements: The number of courses required for a major is not specified, but typically, a major would require more than the 8 credits (2 courses) that can be double-counted with exploratory requirements.
- Minor requirements: Similar to majors, the number of courses required for a minor is not specified, but typically fewer than a major.

Given these requirements, a student might take anywhere from a minimum of 6 courses (exploratory requirements only) to a maximum that would include all core, exploratory, major, and minor requirements, plus any additional courses needed to reach the 128-credit requirement.

The maximum number of courses a student can take in a semester is typically determined by the university's course load policy, which is not provided in the given contexts. Generally, full-time students are expected to take 12 to 18 credits per semester, but this can vary by institution and individual circumstances.

Therefore, without specific institutional policies, it is not possible to definitively state the maximum number of courses a student can take in a semester based solely on the provided information. Students should consult their academic advisor or the university's course load policy for precise information.

### Question 57: How many courses does the university offer?  
### Answer: 

The university offers a variety of courses, including Foundation courses (8 credits), Introductory Language Course (4 credits), Intermediate Language Course (4 credits), Intermediate Courses (8 credits), Advanced (300 Level) Courses (8 credits), 200-level Psychology Courses (various credits), Exploratory courses (32 credits), Core courses (20 credits), and Seminar courses (4 credits). Additionally, there are specific requirements for Quantitative Understanding and Empirical Scientific Thinking (QUEST) courses (4 credits each), and Advanced Seminar (4 credits) for the Bachelor of Science in Integrated Sciences program. The exact number of courses available can vary by department and year, but the university provides a comprehensive range of educational opportunities for students to fulfill their degree requirements.

### Question 58: How can I audit a course?  
### Answer: 

Auditing a course is not officially recognized by the university and does not count towards the student's GPA or credit requirements. To audit a course, students must obtain permission from the course instructor and the Registrar’s Office. The process typically involves filling out an audit form and paying the appropriate fees, if any. It is important to note that auditing a course does not guarantee the student access to all course materials, participation in discussions, or the ability to take exams. Students should consult with their advisor, the course instructor, and the Registrar’s Office for specific procedures and requirements related to auditing a course.

A higher grade will be kept and used in the GPA calculation. The lower grade will be overwritten by the notation “RP”, meaning “repeated”, and remain on the transcript. Credits of the repeated course are counted toward the total attempted credits. A student may not retake the same course for the third time unless he/she failed the second time or if a specific grade is needed to meet the major requirements. Students shall consult with their Academic/Major Advisor about their program plan if they are considering taking the class for a third time. 4.2 Final Grade Release Final grades will be posted by the Registrar’s Office and accessible via the One Stop portal. 4.3 Credit Transfer and Exemptions earned from other universities. ### Question 59: If I'm going for a double major then how many courses can be double count maximum?  
### Answer: 

A student can double count up to 8 credits (two courses) toward both their major requirements. This limit is per each pair of majors (or major and minor) and does not restrict the total number of double-counted credits or courses a student can claim. For example, a student can major in both A and B and minor in C with a total of 6 double-counted courses: two in A and B, two in B and C, and two in A and C. This rule does not apply to courses that are not cross-listed or tagged.

### Question 60: Can you give me the grade translation from alphabetic to numerical?  
### Answer: 

The grade translation from alphabetic to numerical is as follows: A = 4.0, B = 3.0, C = 2.0, D = 1.0, and F = 0.0.

### Question 62: Why and how am I getting academic suspension?  
### Answer: 

Academic Suspension occurs when a student on Academic Probation fails to fulfill all the requirements of their Learning Contract. The process begins with an Academic Warning, which is usually the first level of intervention for students not making satisfactory progress. If the student fails to meet the conditions set during an Academic Progress Meeting and their Learning Contract, they may be placed on Academic Probation. If they continue to fail to meet the requirements, they may then be placed on Academic Suspension.

The specific steps are as follows:

1. **Academic Warning**: The Registrar's Office sends out an Academic Warning to students who are not making satisfactory progress or who fall below a 2.0 GPA. The student is required to attend an Academic Progress Meeting and a Learning Contract is created with specific conditions.

2. **Failure to Meet Conditions**: If the student fails to meet all the requirements of their Learning Contract, they may be placed on Academic Probation.

3. **Academic Probation**: During Academic Probation, the student is typically required to update their Learning Contract and may need to attend additional Academic Progress Meetings.

4. **Continued Failure**: If the student fails to fulfill all the requirements of their Learning Contract while on Academic Probation, they may be placed on Academic Suspension.

5. **Suspension**: The Dean’s Office establishes the conditions the student must meet prior to returning to Fulbright. While on suspension, the student may not visit the campus without permission from the Dean’s Office or the Vice President of Student Affairs.

6. **Maximum Suspension**: A student may be suspended a maximum of two times. A third suspension will result in the cancellation of the student’s enrolment in the program, and the student must apply for re-admission.

The decision to place a student on Academic Suspension is made by the Dean’s Office in consultation with appropriate faculty and staff members, and is typically based on the student's failure to meet academic requirements and/or behavioral issues that represent a present harm to the Fulbright community.

It is important to note that Academic Suspension is a serious academic action and is entered on the student’s transcript as part of their academic record. The student must address the reasons for their suspension and the measures they have taken to address those issues in their application for re-admission. The re-admission decision is also made by the Dean’s Office in consultation with faculty and staff.

In summary, Academic Suspension is a consequence of failing to meet academic and behavioral standards while on Academic Probation. The process involves multiple stages of warnings and interventions before reaching the point of suspension, and the student must meet specific conditions set by the Dean’s Office to return to the program.  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>  

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 11]
to meet all the requirements of their Learning Contract may require an additional Academic Progress Meeting and may be placed on Academic Probation, typically resulting in an updated Learning Contract. DISMISSAL: In all circumstances, Fulbright’s goal is to support students in the completion of their degree. However, dismissal from Fulbright may occur if a suspended student does not fulfill the conditions of suspension, if a student’s behavior represents present harm to others in the Fulbright community, or if a student’s academic performance is unsatisfactory to the degree that continued enrollment is deemed unsupportable. Such decisions are never taken likely, and dismissal occurs only in extraordinary circumstances following multiple written warnings, as determined by the Dean’s Office in consultation with appropriate members of faculty and staff.  

</details> 

### Question 63: How does the P/NP grade work?  
### Answer: 

Students may elect to take a total of 8 credits (typically 2 courses, 4 credits each) in one academic year on a Pass/No Pass basis by completing a Petition to Elect Pass/No Pass Grade form and submitting it to the Registrar’s Office before 4:00 PM on Friday Week 4 of the term (16-week term). Once a student converts a course grade option to Pass/No Pass, the grade option cannot be changed back. Pass/No Pass grades do not apply to core courses. They cannot be used to count toward major/minor requirements. The Registrar’s Office is responsible for converting to the P/NP after receiving the final grade submission by the instructor at the end of the term. A grade of ‘C-’ or higher will be counted as a Pass. Courses graded with a Pass grade can be used to meet prerequisite requirements for next-level courses. Any grade below a ‘C-’ will be counted as a No Pass. No credit is granted for this result. The NP grade will be shown on the Official Transcript. Courses with a NP grade cannot be used to meet prerequisite requirements for next-level courses.

For instance, if course X is cross-listed between major A and major B and tagged by minor C, then X can be used to fulfill the requirements of major A and major B, or major A and minor C, or major B and minor C. ### Question 65: I've failed the GWP due to personal reasons so will it count toward the 144 maximum credits I can have?  
### Answer: 

The maximum number of credits a student can attempt at Fulbright University Vietnam is not explicitly stated in the provided contexts. However, the typical full-time course load is 16 credit hours per term, and students are required to register for no fewer than 12 credit hours and no more than 20 credit hours per term. The contexts mention that credits of repeated courses are counted toward the total attempted credits, and a student must earn a minimum of 128 credit hours to graduate.

The term "attempted credits" refers to the credit hours students enroll in and are recorded in the transcript, regardless of whether they complete the courses or pass them. This includes all courses for which a student receives a grade, including withdrawals (W), incompletes (I), repeats (RP), and failures (F).

Since failing a course results in an 'F' grade, which counts toward attempted credits, each failed course will contribute to the total attempted credits. However, the contexts do not specify a maximum number of attempted credits. Therefore, it is possible for a student to accumulate more than 144 credits if they continue to enroll in and attempt additional courses, including those they fail.

To answer your question directly: The failed GWP course will count toward the total attempted credits, but the maximum number of credits that can be attempted is not specified in the provided information. Therefore, it is possible for the total attempted credits to exceed 144, but there is no explicit cap mentioned in the contexts provided.

In summary, the failed GWP course will count toward the total attempted credits, but the maximum number of attempted credits is not specified in the given information. Students should consult with the Registrar’s Office or their academic advisor for specific policies regarding attempted credits and the maximum number that can be accumulated.

### Question 66: What and how do I get an Academic Warning?  
### Answer: 

An Academic Warning is usually the first level of intervention for students who are not making satisfactory progress toward their degree and/or who fall below a 2.0 GPA, or for other reasons such as failure of attendance, participation, citizenship, or academic integrity. To receive an Academic Warning, the Registrar’s Office sends out an official notification. The student is then required to attend an Academic Progress Meeting where a Learning Contract is created with specific conditions tailored to the individual situation. The Registrar's Office will be informed as a matter of record and follow-up. It is important to note that three official warnings may result in Disciplinary Action, constituting Academic Probation or Academic Suspension.  

<details>

### Question 67: How can I make reservations of rooms for personal usage?  
### Answer: 

You cannot make reservations of rooms for personal usage through the exit checklist provided in the context. The exit checklist is used for checking off tasks that must be completed by a student before leaving the residence halls, such as returning keys and access cards, clearing personal expenses, and submitting any necessary paperwork. To make reservations for personal usage, you would typically contact the residence life office or the housing department of the university directly. They would provide you with the necessary forms or procedures to reserve a room for personal use, such as for hosting a small gathering or event. If you are a student, you would likely need to provide details about the event, the expected number of attendees, and any other relevant information. The university may also have specific policies and fees associated with reserving rooms for personal use. It is important to follow the university's procedures and obtain any necessary permissions or approvals before making such reservations.

### Question 68: Can you provide me with some courses suggestion to take to major in Math?  
### Answer: 

For a major in Applied Mathematics, you should start with the foundation courses, which typically include Linear Algebra, Single Variable Calculus, Introduction to Statistics, and Multivariable Calculus. These courses form the essential elements of modern mathematics and are required for all students pursuing an Applied Mathematics major.

After completing the foundation courses, you should move on to the intermediate courses, which allow you to explore Mathematics in more specific flavors. Sample intermediate courses include Probability, Differential Equations, Discrete Mathematics, and Numerical Analysis. You are required to take at least 2 intermediate courses.

Next, you should select advanced courses to shape your study path into one of the applied fields. There are three main categories with applications in Mathematics: Applied Statistics (Economics, Social Sciences, Mathematical Finance, Bioinformatics…), Engineering Mathematics (Signal processing, Numerical simulation, Inverse problems…), and Data Science (Machine learning, Artificial intelligence …). You are required to take at least 2 advanced courses.

Additionally, you should consider taking at least 2 applied courses, which can be from other majors that emphasize the applications of Mathematics. Sample applied courses could be from Economics, Engineering, Computer Science, or Applied Mathematics itself. Examples include Game Theory, Signal Systems and Control, Introduction to Machine Learning, and Operations Research.

It's also beneficial to participate in research seminars to understand faculty research and recent developments in mathematical research, and you might consider completing a capstone project if it's an option, which allows you to engage in a one-year long research project.

Lastly, consultation with an academic advisor is crucial for course selection to ensure you're on the right track for your major and to tailor your course selection to your specific interests within Applied Mathematics.

Remember, you cannot double count a course for two different requirements, so make sure to plan your course selection carefully to meet all the major requirements without overlapping courses.  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>  

[Source: Major Descriptions and Flowcharts_OfficialVersion.pdf, Page: 17]
course or 1 Elective course, but not both. Pure Mathematics courses: These courses provide the essential abstract concepts and structures that connect various branches of Mathematics and help students understand the adaptability of Mathematics to different types of problems. Students are required to take at least 1 Pure mathematics course. Sample Pure mathematics courses • Abstract Algebra • Real Analysis Research seminars: The seminars provide chances for students to understand about faculty’s research as well as recent developments in mathematical research. Students will also practice basic research skills such as finding resources, writing literature review, and identifying research methodology. Capstone project (optional): This is a one-year long research project to be completed in the last year of study. ### Question 70: I'm currently under a General Work Program for Experiential Learning but the company do not pay me in any form. How can I get the subsidy from Fulbright University?  
### Answer: 

Fulbright University Vietnam's Experiential Learning Program (ELP) offers students the opportunity to engage in credit-bearing experiential learning experiences, which can count toward elective or major requirements with advisor approval. However, the ELP does not appear to provide a subsidy for students who are not paid by the companies or organizations they work with during these experiences.

The ELP is designed to facilitate academic credit-bearing experiences, and while it standardizes and creates opportunities for such learning, it does not guarantee financial compensation for students. The financial aspect of participating in an experiential learning program typically falls on the student or the organization hosting the experience.

If you are not being paid by the company you are working with, you would need to explore other sources of funding. Here are some potential options:

1. **Scholarships and Financial Aid**: Check with Fulbright University Vietnam to see if there are any scholarships, grants, or financial aid available that could support your participation in the ELP.

2. **Part-time Jobs**: Consider looking for a part-time job on campus or in the local community that could provide you with the necessary financial support.

3. **Personal Savings**: Use your personal savings to cover any costs associated with your participation in the ELP.

4. **External Funding**: Look for external funding opportunities such as internships that offer a stipend or other forms of compensation.

5. **Talk to Your Advisor**: Discuss your situation with your academic advisor at Fulbright University Vietnam. They may be able to provide guidance or suggest alternative ways to meet the requirements of the ELP without financial compensation from the host organization.

6. **Non-Academic Sources**: Explore non-academic sources of funding such as family support, community grants, or local non-profits that may offer financial assistance for educational purposes.

Remember to also adhere to the requirements set by Fulbright University Vietnam for participating in the ELP, such as submitting a reflection paper and other documentation as outlined in the leave policy.

In summary, while the ELP at Fulbright University Vietnam provides a structured framework for experiential learning, it does not appear to offer a subsidy for students who are not compensated by their host organizations. Students in such situations should explore various funding options and resources available to them.

### Question 71: Can you give me the process of making a Capstone for graduation?  
### Answer: 

The Capstone process for graduation involves several key stages:

1. **Planning and Proposal**: In the first year (Capstone I), students begin by meeting with their advisors early in the semester to set a regular meeting schedule and expectations for submissions with a timeline. They refine their project proposal and research questions, gather and organize information, and may start initial stages of production (with advisor approval). Midterm submissions typically include a revised project proposal, initial close readings, field journal extracts, and first drafts or excerpts of creative writing.

2. **Research and Development**: During Capstone I, students actively conduct research, focusing on gathering and organizing materials necessary for their project. They are expected to communicate challenges and questions, keep up with their timeline, and manage their expectations regarding what they can accomplish. End-of-term submissions might include a literature review, annotated bibliography, sample chapters, introductions, or selections/excerpts of creative writing, among other materials.

3. **Production and Finalization**: In the second year (Capstone II), students focus more on the production of their project, whether it's writing a thesis, creating a film, or completing a creative project. They continue to work closely with their advisors, refining their work and possibly expanding on their initial submissions. End-of-term submissions for Capstone II typically involve a more substantial piece of work, such as a full literature review (at least five pages or 2,000 words), a complete introduction, or other materials as agreed upon with advisors.

4. **Defense and Evaluation**: The Capstone culminates with a formal defense and a final evaluation by two readers. Students present their completed project and defend their research and findings. The project should demonstrate competency in a primary disciplinary specialization, supported by secondary and tertiary areas if applicable.

5. **Guidance and Flexibility**: Throughout the process, students work with a primary advisor and, to a lesser extent, a secondary advisor. The format and length of projects are flexible within certain limits, allowing for a wide range of disciplines and project types. Students should consult with their advisors early in their junior year to ensure the feasibility of their proposed project.

6. **Learning Outcomes**: The Capstone is designed to help students demonstrate specific competencies, such as analyzing historical, cultural, social, and contemporary issues with a critical interdisciplinary approach, and mastering research and analytical skills using primary and secondary sources.

The Capstone experience is a comprehensive and individualized opportunity for students to apply and synthesize their knowledge and skills in a meaningful and coherent way, preparing them for further academic or professional pursuits.  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>  

[Source: History Capstone Handbook - 2025-26.pdf, Page: 9]

However, if you wish to graduate with distinction—an honorable goal— but you don’t particularly enjoy doing independent research, you may want to skip the Capstone and focus on GPA honors instead! Students work with a primary advisor and, to a lesser extent, a secondary advisor. The format and length of projects is flexible, within certain limits. ### Question 73: How can I graduate with Honor or lie within the top GPA?  
### Answer: 

To graduate with Honors or achieve a high GPA, you need to focus on several key areas:

1. **Academic Performance**: Maintain a high GPA by consistently achieving grades of A or A-. This means performing well in all your courses, especially those that count towards your major or minor. Pay close attention to the grading scale and understand the weight of each course in your overall GPA calculation.

2. **Capstone Project**: Excel in your Capstone project by ensuring it distinguishes itself through originality of the research question, creativity in implementation, and diligence in effort applied. Aim for a score that places you in the Honors Pass category (4.5-5 out of 5), which corresponds to a grade of A or A-.

3. **Defense**: While the defense itself does not impact your final score, use it as an opportunity to demonstrate your knowledge and understanding of your project. This can help build confidence in your advisors and reviewers, potentially influencing their overall assessment.

4. **Course Selection**: Choose courses wisely, especially those that are challenging and relevant to your field of study. High-level courses can help boost your GPA if you perform well in them.

5. **Time Management**: Manage your time effectively to balance coursework, capstone project, and any other commitments. This will help you avoid last-minute stress and ensure consistent performance.

6. **Seek Feedback**: Actively seek feedback from your advisors and peers. Use this feedback to improve your work and address any weaknesses in your understanding or performance.

7. **Repeat Courses Strategically**: If you receive a grade below a 'C-' in a core course or a course that counts towards your major/minor, you must repeat it. Use this opportunity to improve your grade and thus your GPA.

8. **Stay Informed**: Understand the university's policies regarding grading, GPA calculation, and course repetition. Be aware of deadlines and requirements for graduating with honors.

9. **Stay Motivated**: Keep yourself motivated and focused on your academic and career goals. Celebrate small victories along the way to maintain your momentum.

Remember, graduating with honors is a selective process. While meeting the basic requirements is important, exceeding expectations through your academic performance and capstone project is what will set you apart and potentially earn you an Honors Pass. 

It's also crucial to note that the GPA calculation does not include courses for which you've received a P/NP, I, W, or RP grade. Therefore, strategically choosing courses that offer letter grades can also impact your GPA positively.

Lastly, always consult with your academic advisor for personalized advice and to ensure you're on the right track to meet your academic goals.  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>  

[Source: Economics Capstone Guideline_AY2025-2026.pdf, Page: 11]

The Defense At the end of Capstone II, you are required to participate in a formal defense of your project, regardless of what format you have chosen. By default, this is a private defense, involving only the primary advisor, secondary advisor, and Capstone student. The basic format is as follows: • You give a 15-20 minute

[Source: History Capstone Handbook - 2025-26.pdf, Page: 12]
a scale of 1-5 points against the common History rubric (see below) and will return scores for each major component on the rubric. You will receive one of the following results based on your final score: ● Pass: 3.0-4.4 ● Honors Pass: 4.5-5 ● No Pass: 1.0-2.9 Each student will defend their project toward the end of Capstone II. The defense is required. However, the defense is not scored, and it does not contribute to the final score. The advising team may expect you to incorporate feedback from the defense into your final submission, but that feedback will not directly impact your final score. For more information on the defense, see the “Defense” section below. Tip: A “Pass” on Capstone II is a very respectable result! Of course, most student hope to pass with honors, but the Honors Pass is reserved for projects that exceed expectations. Some students have been surprised to find that they met all the basic requirements and yet received only a “Pass.” However, our philosophy is that the Honors Pass is more

### Answer:

For example, an exploratory course can also be counted as a major course for the corresponding major. However, the number of double-counted credits is often limited (e.g., see subsections 13.2 and 13.3). Additionally, any particular course can only be used to satisfy at most two requirements. For instance, if course X is cross-listed between major A and major B and tagged by minor C, then X can be used to fulfill the requirements of major A and major B, or major A and minor C, or major B and minor C. Triple counting is not allowed. 13.9  Maximal Length of Study Fulbright follows MOET regulations, which state that the maximum time to complete the program cannot exceed double the standard

### Question 76: How many types of leave of absence are there and how can I have one?  
### Answer: 

There are three types of leave of absence: personal leave, medical leave, and administrative leave of absence. To have one, you must complete and submit the Undergraduate Leave of Absence form, along with supporting documents, before the last day of class. For personal leave, you must submit the form at least three weeks prior to the leave, or as soon as possible if circumstances do not permit an application within this period. For medical leave, you must submit the form along with the Fulbright Impact Assessment form and all medical supporting documents before the last day of class. For administrative leave of absence, you must have an outstanding student account balance in an enrolled term without informing the university, and the leave will be automatically granted.

### Question 77: How can I report violation of Academic environment anonymously?  
### Answer: 

Fulbright University Vietnam has established a process for reporting violations of academic integrity anonymously. Here's how you can report such violations:

1. **Use the Online Reporting System**: Fulbright University Vietnam likely has an online system for reporting academic integrity violations. This system allows students to submit reports confidentially.

2. **Contact the Office of Academic Affairs**: You can also report violations directly to the Office of Academic Affairs. They may have a confidential hotline or email address set up specifically for such reports.

3. **Speak to a Trusted Advisor**: If you're uncomfortable using the online system or contacting the office directly, you can speak to a trusted advisor, such as a counselor or academic advisor. They can help facilitate the reporting process while maintaining your anonymity.

4. **Follow the University's Procedures**: When submitting a report, make sure to follow the university's established procedures for reporting academic integrity violations. This usually involves providing as much detail as possible without revealing your identity.

5. **Check the University's Website**: The university's website may have specific instructions on how to report academic integrity violations anonymously. It's always a good idea to check there first.

6. **Seek Confidentiality**: When reporting, make it clear that you wish to remain anonymous. The university should respect your wishes and handle the report confidentially.

Remember, the university's primary goal is to maintain a fair and honest academic environment. Reporting violations of academic integrity is an important step in upholding these standards, and doing so anonymously can help protect the reporter from potential retaliation.

It's important to note that while anonymity is respected, the university will still investigate the reported violation thoroughly and take appropriate action. The university's commitment to academic integrity is paramount, and they are likely to have a dedicated team to handle such matters discreetly and effectively.

In summary, to report a violation of academic integrity anonymously at Fulbright University Vietnam, you should:

- Use the online reporting system or contact the Office of Academic Affairs.
- Speak to a trusted advisor if you prefer not to contact the office directly.
- Follow the university's procedures for reporting.
- Make it clear you wish to remain anonymous.
- Check the university's website for specific instructions.

The university will handle the report confidentially and take appropriate action to maintain academic integrity.

### Question 79: What is the school's policy on academic dishonesty, and how are violations handled?  
### Answer: 

The school's policy on academic dishonesty is outlined in Fulbright University Vietnam’s Code of Academic Integrity. Violations of academic integrity are taken seriously, and students are expected to meet all the requirements of their Learning Contract. Failure to do so may result in an additional Academic Progress Meeting, being placed on Academic Probation, and potentially updating the Learning Contract. Academic Probation typically lasts for a term, and if the student fails to fulfill all the requirements, they may be placed on Academic Suspension. Suspensions are recorded on the student's transcript and typically last for one term or one academic year. A student may be suspended a maximum of two times. After two suspensions, a student will not be suspended a third time but will instead face more severe consequences, such as being dismissed from the university. While on suspension, students may not visit the campus without permission from the Dean’s Office or the Vice President of Student Affairs, usually for attending relevant meetings. The use of generative AI is also subject to the regulations described in the Code of Academic Integrity, indicating that the school expects students to adhere to these standards in all aspects of their academic work.

### Question 80: What are the policies regarding retaking exams or improving grades?  
### Answer: 

Students may repeat a course if they have received a grade of ‘D+’, ‘D’, ‘F’, or NP for the course or have withdrawn and received a grade of ‘W’. In special cases, a student may request to repeat a course after having received a grade higher than a ‘D+’ following consultation with their academic/major advisor, the course instructor, and the Registrar’s Office. Considerations will include course availability and capacity in the next academic term or next academic year. A higher grade will be kept and used in the GPA calculation. The lower grade will be overwritten by the notation “RP”, meaning “repeated”, and remain on the transcript. Credits of the repeated course are counted toward the total attempted credits. A student may not retake the same course for the third time unless he/she failed the second time or if a specific grade is needed to meet the major requirements. Students shall consult with their Academic/Major Advisor about their program plan if they are considering taking the class for a third time.  

<details>

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 10]

A higher grade will be kept and used in the GPA calculation. The lower grade will be overwritten by the notation “RP”, meaning “repeated”, and remain on the transcript. Credits of the repeated course are counted toward the total attempted credits. A student may not retake the same course for the third time unless he/she failed the second time or if a specific grade is needed to meet the major requirements. Otherwise, the Registrar's Office organizes a panel review with the Dean’s Office for an independent evaluation of the grade. The grade determined after this evaluation shall be the final grade. ### Question 81: How does the university accommodate students with chronic illnesses or personal emergencies that affect attendance?  
### Answer: 

The university acknowledges that sometimes students will experience exceptional circumstances, such as exceptional medical circumstances (e.g., hospitalization or incapacitation through injury, illness, or mental health crisis), close bereavement, being a victim of a serious crime, or having disabilities for which reasonable adjustments are not yet in place. For medical leaves, students must submit the Undergraduate Leave of Absence form and the Fulbright Impact Assessment form, along with all medical supporting documents, before the last day of class. The leave duration is determined in conjunction with a health services professional, the Registrar’s Office, and the student. Indefinite leaves are not permitted.

The university also allows administrative leaves of absence for students with outstanding student account balances without informing the university. Additionally, students may be excused from classes for university-sponsored events, but only upon request by faculty or supervising staff, and with the approval of the Office of the Dean and Deans of Students. Students must be in good academic standing and not on probation to be excused from the class.

Instructors are expected to outline attendance expectations at the beginning of each term and handle absences due to exceptional circumstances appropriately. For students prevented by illness or medical condition from pursuing a full course of study, a letter from a licensed medical doctor is required, detailing the medical problem, treatment, and a recommendation for when the student may resume taking full-time coursework.

In summary, the university accommodates students with chronic illnesses or personal emergencies affecting attendance through medical leaves, administrative leaves, and excused absences for university-sponsored events, provided the necessary documentation and approvals are obtained.

### Question 82: Are students allowed to use AI tools like ChatGPT for assignments? If so, under what conditions?  
### Answer: 

Students are allowed to use AI tools like ChatGPT for their assignments, but under strict conditions to ensure academic integrity and ethical use. The use of generative AI in creative projects must be fully documented, including the reasons for its utilization, the specific elements it contributes to the project, the tools and platforms employed, the methodology for incorporating AI, and how ethical and copyright concerns are addressed. This documentation is crucial for transparency and to highlight the originality and human contributions to the project.

The use of generative AI is subject to Fulbright University Vietnam’s Code of Academic Integrity, which strictly prohibits presenting content created by generative AI as one's own work. This includes text, images, or any other content generated by AI. Students must obtain Institutional Review Board (IRB) approval for their research involving human subjects, ensuring that data protection and ethical research practices are adhered to.

Furthermore, students must submit comprehensive documentation of their AI usage as part of their Capstone projects, detailing every aspect of how AI was employed. This documentation is required to be submitted at specific milestones throughout the Capstone process, such as the end of Capstone I.

In summary, while students are allowed to use AI tools like ChatGPT for their assignments, they must do so transparently, ethically, and in compliance with the university's academic integrity policies and IRB requirements. Failure to adhere to these guidelines can result in delays, suspension of projects, or even failure of Capstone I and/or Capstone II.

### Question 83: Where can I get support for mental health treatment?  
### Answer: 

Students can access support for mental health treatment through several avenues provided by the educational institution. Firstly, they can participate in the Mental Health First Aid Training Program, which offers training and resources to better understand and support mental health issues. Additionally, a Community Survey on Mental Health Needs could help identify local resources and services that are available and accessible to students. The Public Art Project on Mental Health Themes might also serve as a source of awareness and support, as well as the Resource Guide for Local Mental Health Services, which would provide comprehensive information on where and how to seek help.

Furthermore, the institution offers support through Therapeutic Gardening or Nature Programs, which can be beneficial for mental health. Students can also receive support through the Capstone by Applied Project, where they might develop a project focused on mental health support and treatment, with the guidance of academic and possibly non-academic advisors.

Extensive documentation of the process through which these projects are developed is required, and a final Evaluation Report must demonstrate the project’s relevance to psychological principles, its potential or observed impact, and evidence of rigorous evaluation. This report should also provide insights or recommendations for future application or research.

In terms of direct support, students may also have access to labs, tools, and software relevant to their project work, as well as support for learning new software or techniques necessary for their mental health projects. Limited funding for materials and resources is also available for projects, which can be crucial for implementing mental health initiatives.

Non-academic advisors, such as mental health practitioners or community organizers, can also be involved in the project to provide additional expertise and support. These advisors must be approved by the student's primary and secondary academic advisors from the Psychology program.

Overall, the educational institution provides a comprehensive support system for students seeking mental health treatment, including training programs, surveys, art projects, resource guides, therapeutic programs, and academic and industry mentorship opportunities.

### Question 85: Where can I find resources to facilitate my graduate program after Fulbright undergraduate?  
### Answer: 

After completing their undergraduate program at Fulbright, students interested in pursuing graduate studies with a specific focus on Vietnam and related fields can look into several resources. Here are some potential avenues:

1. **Graduate Programs**: Fulbright alumni can explore graduate programs offered by universities around the world that specialize in Vietnam Studies, History, Asian Studies, or related fields. These programs often provide in-depth knowledge and research opportunities related to Vietnam.

2. **Vietnam Studies Centers and Institutes**: Many universities and research institutions have centers or institutes dedicated to the study of Vietnam. These centers often offer graduate programs, research opportunities, and access to specialized resources.

3. **Academic Journals and Publications**: Engaging with academic journals and publications focused on Vietnam Studies can provide valuable insights and resources for graduate studies. Alumni can also consider contributing to these publications, which can help establish their academic credentials.

4. **Networking**: Alumni can leverage their Fulbright network to connect with professionals and academics working in the field of Vietnam Studies. Networking can lead to mentorship opportunities, research collaborations, and guidance on graduate programs.

5. **Online Courses and MOOCs**: Online platforms offer courses on various aspects of Vietnam, including history, culture, politics, and economics. These can serve as a foundation for further graduate study.

6. **Scholarships and Financial Aid**: Fulbright alumni should explore scholarships and financial aid opportunities specifically designed for graduate studies in Vietnam-related fields. These can include Fulbright scholarships for graduate study, as well as other national and international awards.

7. **Research Institutions and NGOs**: Engaging with research institutions, non-governmental organizations (NGOs), and international organizations that work on Vietnam-related issues can provide practical experience and potential pathways to graduate studies.

By utilizing these resources, Fulbright alumni can build a strong foundation for their graduate studies, ensuring they are well-prepared to contribute to the field of Vietnam Studies.

### Question 86: Can you give me information about teaching assistant program right now?  
### Answer: 

Fulbright’s Experiential Learning Program (ELP) exists to create, standardize, and facilitate academic credit-bearing experiential learning experiences. All full-time, undergraduate, degree-seeking students must complete at least 04 credits and a maximum of 12 credits under the ELP as a graduation requirement. Current ELP streams are housed within Academic Affairs (AA), Career Services (CS), and Center for Entrepreneurship and Innovation (CEI), where each team is responsible for administrative and academic duties of their respective stream(s). The ELP operates within Fulbright’s undergraduate program’s standard operating procedures and is developed in consultation with undergraduate program leadership and faculty. For more details, please refer here https://onestop.fulbright.edu.vn/s/article/Experiential-Learning-Program. However, this does not specifically mention a teaching assistant program. The ELP provides experiential learning opportunities, but it does not necessarily equate to a teaching assistant program, which typically involves working under faculty supervision to assist in teaching courses and may not necessarily be credit-bearing. For information on teaching assistantships, students should consult directly with their academic department or the Office of Academic Affairs at Fulbright University Vietnam.

### Question 87: Where can I get the assist for internship?  
### Answer: 

The primary advisor is your first point of contact for assistance with your internship. They should hold expertise relevant to your project, and will be the primary reviewer of your work in progress. They will be the person with whom you meet most frequently for guidance and support. If your primary advisor is unable to continue, the secondary advisor will take over. Additionally, students have access to labs, tools, and software relevant to their project work, and support will be provided for learning new software or techniques where necessary. Industry mentors may also be available for project guidance, helping bridge academic learning and industry practice. They could participate in grading as either a secondary advisor or a member of the capstone panel. For logistical support specific to the internship, such as organizing events or managing liability concerns, students must notify the relevant faculty and university functionaries well in advance. Funding and material procurement policies will also be provided to ensure that projects have access to the necessary resources. The major capstone coordinator will consult with students if their first or second preference for advisors cannot be accommodated due to faculty workload, to determine an appropriate solution.

### Question 88: How can I get the information of campus usage for events so that I can have proper place to study without getting interrupted?  
### Answer: 

To get information on campus usage for events so that you can study without interruption, you should follow these steps:

1. **Contact the Registrar’s Office**: Reach out to the Registrar’s Office at your university. They maintain records of all events and activities happening on campus, including room bookings and event schedules. They can provide you with a calendar or schedule of events, which will help you identify times and locations where events are taking place.

2. **Check the University Website**: Many universities have a dedicated section on their website for events and calendars. This is often updated regularly with the latest information on upcoming events, including dates, times, and locations. Checking this regularly can help you plan your study schedule accordingly.

3. **Use the University’s Event Scheduling System**: If your university has an online system for scheduling events, you can use this to see what events are planned and where they will take place. This system is usually accessible to students and can provide real-time updates.

4. **Consult with Academic Advisors or Departmental Staff**: Your academic advisor or the staff in your department may also have information about upcoming events that could impact your study space. They can provide insights and possibly even suggest alternative study locations during busy event times.

5. **Join Student Groups or Forums**: Sometimes, student groups or forums have discussions about campus events and how they might affect study spaces. Joining these groups can provide you with timely information and tips from fellow students.

6. **Direct Communication with Event Organizers**: If you know the specific event that might interfere with your study, consider reaching out directly to the organizers. They might be able to provide you with more detailed information about the event’s schedule and potential impact on study spaces.

By following these steps, you should be able to gather the necessary information about campus usage for events, allowing you to plan your study schedule effectively and minimize interruptions. Remember, it’s also important to communicate your needs to your instructors and advisors, as they may be able to offer additional support or alternative arrangements.

**Note**: The provided answer assumes a general university setting. Specific procedures may vary by institution, so it’s always best to refer to your university’s specific policies and resources.

### Question 89: I used to take an undergraduate program at another university and I want to transfer credit of studied courses at that university. What should I do?  
### Answer: 

When applying for Credit Transfer, a student is responsible for providing all the information that is requested about the course(s) for which they are applying to transfer. Information may include but not limited to course curriculum, learning outcomes, course content, and assessments. To apply for Credit Transfer or an Exemption, students should submit an Application for Credit Transfer, along with the required information on the course(s), to the Office of the Registrar. The Registrar will coordinate with the Office of the Dean to determine whether credit or an exemption is justified. Credit Transfer from A-level, Advanced Placement (AP), International Baccalaureate (IB), and Cambridge International Exam (CIE) courses will be determined according to the AP/IB/CIE Credit Policy (in development). For more information, please refer to the Transfer Credit policy_Final_V3.0 published in the One Stop portal.

### Question 90: What are the policies of CWP?  
### Answer: 

The policies of CWP (Capstone project at Fulbright University Vietnam) include the following:

1. **Project Scope and Definition**: The capstone project should focus on practical, real-world applications in human-centered engineering or contribute knowledge in scientific and engineering fields through research activities. It should demonstrate originality, a sound research process, and feasibility for practical application, with clear objectives.

2. **Project Constraints**: The project must account for constraints within the reasonable resources that students and advisors can mobilize for conducting research and development activities. It should consider environmental, economic, health, sustainability, and manufacturability factors. Guidance will be provided to integrate these factors systematically.

3. **Engineering Standards**: Students should follow widely accepted research practices and appropriate engineering standards to deliver the expected project.

4. **Logistical Support**: If the project requires logistical arrangements outside the university’s typical academic scope, students must notify the Psychology faculty and relevant Fulbright University functionaries well in advance. This includes:

   - Events organized by the student in partnership with groups inside or outside Fulbright University Vietnam.
   - Applied projects taking place on the FUV campus (e.g., organizing an exhibition, producing a documentary).
   - Any project that may raise liability concerns for the student or the institution.

5. **Application Process**: The application process follows the UG program policies as stated explicitly in the Capstone Guidelines.

6. **Revision and Updates**: The Academic Policy is reviewed annually for the forthcoming academic year, and any substantive changes are reviewed by the Dean’s Office and submitted to the Provost for approval. The approved policy is published and available in the One Stop portal.

7. **Additional Requirements and Exceptions**: Majors and minors may have additional policies and requirements, which should be clearly stated in the major’s description and flowchart or handbook. Any exception to the policy must be approved by the major coordinator and the Dean’s Office.

These policies aim to provide structure, support, and flexibility for students undertaking capstone projects, ensuring they are practical, feasible, and adhere to academic and ethical standards. The university is committed to innovation and continuous improvement, reflected in the regular updates to these policies.

### Question 91: I'm a student athlete then how can I bypass all of physical education courses required to graduate?  
### Answer: 

As a student athlete, you may be exempt from some or all of the physical education courses required to graduate, depending on the university's policies and your specific athletic involvement. Here's how you might navigate this:

1. **Athletic Department Exemption**: Often, universities have agreements with their athletic departments that allow student athletes to fulfill their physical education requirements through their athletic participation. This means that the physical activity and training you engage in as part of your athletic program can count towards your PE requirement.

2. **Consult with the Athletic Department**: Reach out to your athletic department to understand the specific policies regarding PE course exemptions for student athletes. They can guide you on the necessary steps and documentation required to be exempted.

3. **Documentation**: You may need to provide documentation of your athletic participation, such as a schedule of your training sessions, competitions, and the hours dedicated to your sport. This documentation can be used to justify why you do not need to take additional PE courses.

4. **Dean of Students or Registrar’s Office**: In some cases, you might need to submit a request to the Dean of Students or the Registrar’s Office for an exemption. They will likely require verification from the athletic department.

5. **Academic Advising**: Consult with your academic advisor to ensure that you are on track to meet all graduation requirements, including any potential substitutions or waivers for PE courses.

6. **Review University Policies**: Familiarize yourself with the university's policies regarding PE requirements and exemptions for student athletes. This information is usually available in the academic catalog or on the university's website.

7. **Alternative Courses**: If you cannot be exempted from all PE courses, consider taking alternative courses that can count towards your degree requirements. For example, you might take additional courses in your major or minor, or explore electives that interest you.

8. **Stay Informed**: Policies and requirements can change, so it's important to stay informed and check in regularly with the relevant university offices to ensure you are meeting all current requirements.

Remember, while being a student athlete can provide certain exemptions, it's crucial to maintain a balanced academic and athletic schedule to ensure success in both areas. Always consult with the appropriate university officials to understand your specific situation and requirements.

All students for whom English is not their first language must take an English Assessment Test required by NECHE Accreditation. 13.8  Double Counting In satisfying degree requirements, some courses can be double-counted. For example, an exploratory course can also be counted as a major course for the corresponding major. However, the number of double-counted credits is often limited (e.g., see subsections 13.2 and 13.3). Additionally, any particular course can only be used to satisfy at most two requirements. For instance, if course X is cross-listed between major A and major B and tagged by minor C, then X can be used to fulfill the requirements of major A and major B, or major A and minor C, or major B and minor C. Triple counting is not allowed. 13.9  Maximal Length of Study Fulbright follows MOET regulations, which state that the maximum time to complete the program cannot exceed double the standard

maintaining the full-time enrollment status of registering for a minimum of 12 credits per term. • Course Withdrawal: There are two Withdrawal periods per term. The first period starts after 4:00 PM Friday, Week 2 to 4:00 PM Friday, Week 4. During this time, students can request to withdraw from the course without any consequence. The second period starts after 4:00 PM Friday, Week 4 to 4:00 PM Friday, Week 8. Attempted credits and a “W” grade will be recorded on the transcript for the course(s) from which students withdraw in this period. This grade does not affect a student’s GPA. In order to withdraw from a course, students need to submit the Course Withdrawal form to the Registrar’s Office, following the above timeline. A student who withdraws from a course beyond the halfway point of that course (end of week 4 for an 8-week course or end of week 8 for a full-term course) automatically receives a grade of ‘F’, which will affect the student’s GPA. Students need to complete 8 credits (2 courses at the 100-level or 200-level) chosen from each of the three categories: E1- Arts and Humanities, E2-Social Sciences, and STEM. No course can be taken to fulﬁll more than one category. At most, 8 exploratory credits can be taken as Pass/No Pass. Up to two 8 exploratory credits (2 courses) can be counted towards the student’s major requirements. If a student has more than one major, the 8-credit limit is independently applied to each major. Students should aim to complete the Exploratory requirements by the end of their second year. Notes: •  Class of 2023 students are exempted from the Exploratory requirements. • There are four exploratory categories for the students of classes of 2024, 2025, 2026, and 2027: E1-Arts and Humanities, E2-Social Sciences, E3- Sciences and Engineering, and E4-Mathematics and Computing. • Class of 2024 students need to complete 16 credits (4 courses) to fulﬁll their

### Question 92: I'm currently a third year student and moved out of university dormitory after first year but now I'm struggling to find a new accommodation so can I register again for dormitory and will I still have the financial support?  
### Answer: 

If you're struggling to find new accommodation, you might consider reaching out to the Residential Life office at your university. They may have resources or suggestions for temporary housing while you search for a more permanent solution. As for registering again for the dormitory, most universities have a process for re-applying or re-joining the dormitory system, especially if you're a current student experiencing difficulties.

Regarding financial support, it typically depends on the university's policies and availability of funds. Some universities offer financial assistance or support for students facing housing difficulties, but this is not guaranteed and would need to be discussed with the relevant financial aid or student services office.

To proceed, you should:

1. Contact the Residential Life office to inquire about the process for re-applying to the dormitory and any available temporary housing options.
2. Reach out to the Financial Aid office to discuss your situation and inquire about any potential financial support or assistance programs.
3. Complete any necessary paperwork or applications for re-admission to the dormitory.
4. Keep open communication with university staff to ensure you're aware of all available resources and support.

Remember, each university has its own policies and procedures, so it's important to engage directly with the relevant offices to get accurate and personalized information.

### Question 93: Can you give me the procedure to request for funds to support club activities?  
### Answer: 

Students should first consult with their club advisors to understand the budgeting and purchasing policies. Then, they should prepare a detailed proposal outlining the purpose, budget, and expected outcomes of the club activities they wish to support. This proposal should be submitted to the Office of Student Affairs or the Financial Aid Office, depending on the specific funding sources available at FUV. It is important to adhere to any deadlines for funding requests and to provide any additional documentation that may be required, such as receipts or letters of support from faculty or other stakeholders. The review process may involve a committee that assesses the proposals based on their feasibility, impact, and alignment with the university's goals. Once the proposal is approved, the funds will be disbursed according to the agreed-upon terms, and students will be responsible for managing the budget and providing any necessary reports or updates on the progress of the club activities. It is crucial to maintain clear and transparent records of all expenditures to ensure accountability and compliance with university policies.

### Question 94: How many ways are there provided by the university can I get the support in my academic path?  
### Answer: 

There are several ways the university provides support in the academic path of students. Firstly, students have access to labs, tools, and software relevant to their project work, which is crucial for practical learning and application. Secondly, support is provided for learning new software or techniques where necessary, ensuring that students are not left behind in terms of technological skills. Thirdly, mentorship and industry involvement are available, with industry mentors potentially serving as secondary advisors for capstone projects or participating in grading as members of the capstone panel. This industry involvement helps bridge the gap between academic learning and real-world practice. Fourthly, limited funding for materials and resources is available for projects, which alleviates some of the financial burden on students. Lastly, logistical support is provided for projects that require arrangements outside the typical academic scope, with students needing to notify the Psychology faculty and relevant university functionaries well in advance. This comprehensive support system is designed to ensure students have the resources and guidance they need to succeed in their academic endeavors.
